This was extended to consider the ways in which people lived with things and how they used them to negotiate the world both inside and outside their homes.29 The emphasis on the agency of the consumer, combined with Attfield’s assertion that design was an everyday series of practices, allowed academics to consider the seemingly banal daily activities of homemaking, decorating, making, cooking and getting dressed as significant exercises in creativity.30 Likewise, for those working within the field of material culture, Wild Things emphasized the significance of the materiality of objects and the contribution design made in relation to the consumption of objects.

By the 2010 publication of Kjetil Fallan’s Design History: Understanding Theory and Method (Berg) the methods and approaches used by Attfield in Wild Things had become very much part of the established techniques of the design historian. Attfield’s championing of material culture, and particularly that emanating from social anthropology, Fallon notes,31 was a means of expanding the field (a la Rosalind Krauss)32 of the objects of ‘design’, while opening the potential for their study in more holistic and inclusive ways.33 This, of course, was not foolproof, nor did it offer a simple, all-encompassing or deterministic method for the study of all objects. And while Fallon criticizes the lack of emphasis on pre-production, Attfield herself was well aware of the problems of disparity to be encountered in the study of such a mass of things, and an inability of some studies to address materiality per se (ibid).

Fallon’s intimation that Wild Things was less interested in design and the design process than the interrelationships between people and their things is both fair and flawed. First, the text discusses design and its histories in an unsurpassed manner hitherto its publication, while offering case studies of the design process and the imprint of the designer within the first chapters. References to traditional furniture makers (Clarkes of High Wycombe) through to knit designer Kaffe Fassett offer insights into twentieth-century design and design practice that, at the time of writing, would have been considered ‘unfashionable’ or of marginal interest. The focus on essentially ‘craft’ production as ‘design’ in these examples highlighted the ways in which design history could be expanded to include a variety of objects and makers outside of its remit, indicating that its language would be enriched and even underpinned. For example, by considering notions of authenticity, the reproduction and even non-design, Attfield emphasized the ways in which ‘good design’ could be considered as having a wider and longer-lasting impact on the visual and material, or indeed designed world, than had previously be imagined. Design was not just about the world or word of designers, nor was it merely a lofty commentary on ‘taste’.

Likewise, in a brave attempt to make connections between production and consumption (beyond point of sale) Attfield considers the consumers, owners and users, and even those who recycle, hide, waste and throw away of objects (as Appadurai had),34 as makers, not merely of meaning, as this would be too reductive, but as agents navigating a life through living with things. There is no doubt that this consideration offered a new depth to studies of design that was exciting, and boundless, that aimed to add more voices to the discussion of what objects ‘mean’ and how they are ‘used’. This was a move towards democratization, in which the designer, the object and the consumer were considered contributors to the significance of things in everyday life. Studies in popular culture and particularly fashion studies had started to address these issues in the 1970s, with discussions of subcultural consumption and the relationship between objects and the construction of both group and personal identity. Contextually, as identity and lifestyle became buzzwords in relation to the positioning, marketing and selling commodities in the post-war period, consumers were given a sense of agency through a flexible, non-reductive combination of personal choice and lifestyle marketing, which promoted bespoke systems of personalization through processes of bricolage and display tactics, which might be considered examples of everyday creativity. Attfield noted: ‘The way people objectify that desire in the making, unmaking and remaking of their material world is pervasive activity in people’s lives one way or another,’35 emphasizing the ways in which design informed daily life in varied ways.

Wild Things is a book that connects; it joins the dots between disciplines linking modes of thought and discussion in new and innovative ways. By connecting design history with everyday objects and the field of material culture, the book connects people to things in a way that re-evaluates the significance of objects in daily life. By privileging design, but only as one category within the field of material culture (things with attitude), and by valorizing these objects through processes that include knowledge, skill and application, design discourse here widens to include previously marginalized design practices such as craft. In doing this, she also considers what constitutes a designer, ranging from the most visible and professional examples through to unclassified acts of design, such as getting dressed or home decorating, the success of which demonstrate skills greatly admired by peers.

Likewise, objects hitherto considered non-design, reproductions, novelty or ‘poor taste’ are brought into the discussion as examples of cultural critiques of the democratization of design or players in object–person relationships. It challenges the notions of hierarchy, in terms of discipline and system of objects that span concepts of taste through to those associated with net worth and the moral judgements that accompany such a system, in order to reclassify, reorder and reassess the meaning of things. This, of course, was not a reductive exercise to establish a language of things; it was a starting point for the discussion of the interplay between people and their things, and the ways in which these relationships helped to negotiate daily life in a variety of ways. Attfield wrote: ‘This study is situated at the dynamic point of interplay between animate and inanimate worlds in order to look beyond the material world of mere things in themselves and reconsider their complex role in the relationship between objects and subjects’.36 Wild Things was never intended

to be the definitive word on design as material culture, but a starting point in which the canon could be questioned and expanded to meet the needs of a new century of objects, and one that included, if not privileged, object–person relationships.

The value of Wild Things is multifarious; from expanding a field, to crossing disciplines, from challenging the canon through to empowering mute objects and voices to speak, the book offers a clear contextual survey of thinking and writing about objects at a particular time, while challenging convention through (sometimes heartfelt) testimony accompanied with specific examples. It offered an introduction to how to address, if not contain disorder; disobedient objects and messy lives clutter and chatter, with their inextricable entanglements, in order to demystify the stuff of everyday life and to prove there was no singly way of understanding the world and the things within it.

—Jo Turney, Winchester School of Art, University of Southampton
